Hudud — Fixed Penalties
beginner
10 min
Definition of Hadd (Fixed Punishment) in Hanafi Law
Linguistic and legal definition of hadd with distinctions from tazir and qisas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the legal definition of hadd, distinguish it from tazir and qisas, and explain why hadd cannot be waived after establishment before a judge.

Core Idea
Hadd is a fixed, unforgivable punishment for a right of Allah, distinct from tazir and qisas.

This lesson introduces the concept of hadd (fixed punishment) as defined in the Hanafi school. Understanding this definition is crucial because it determines whether a penalty can be mitigated or waived. We will examine how Hanafi jurists distinguish hadd from other forms of punishment and why intercession is not allowed once the case reaches the judge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic meaning of hadd
The text starts with the literal meaning 'prevention' (al-man') and gives examples like gatekeepers and jailers. This shows the root concept of restricting action.
The same word is used for boundaries (ḥudūd al-diyār) because they prevent encroachment.
2
Distinguish legal definition from linguistic
Legally, hadd is defined as 'a fixed punishment prescribed by Allah' (ʿuqūbah muqaddarah li-Llāh taʿālā). The key is 'fixed' (muqaddarah) and 'for Allah' (li-Llāh).
This excludes tazir (discretionary punishment) due to lack of fixation (li-ʿadam al-taqdīr).
3
Exclude non-hadd punishments
Tazir is excluded because it is not fixed; the range of 3-39 lashes does not fix a specific number. Qisas is excluded because it is a right of the servant (ḥaqq al-ʿabd).
Some scholars include qisas under hadd, but the well-known (mashhūr) Hanafi view excludes it.
4
Explore the consequence of classification
Once the cause of hadd is established before the judge (thubūt sababihī ʿinda al-ḥākim), the punishment cannot be waived. Intercession (shafāʿah) is impermissible because it would drop a right of Allah.
This contrasts with qisas, where the victim or family can forgive.
5
Note the ikhtilaf on qisas
Some hold that qisas is also called hadd because it is a fixed punishment. The text states that according to the well-known view, qisas is not called hadd, but acknowledges the alternative.
This difference affects whether the punishment can be forgiven after reaching the judge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم كتاب الحدود الحد في اللغة المنع ومنه سمي البواب حداداً لمنعه الناس عن الدخول والسجان حداداً لمنعه عن الخروج، وحدود الديار نهاياتها لمنعها عن دخول ملك الغير فيها وخروج بعضها إليه، وسمي الجامع المانع حداً لأنه يجمع معنى الشيء ويمنع دخول غيره فيه، وسميت العقوبات الخالصة حدوداً لأنها موانع من ارتكاب أسبابها معاودة وحدود الله محارمه لأنها ممنوع عنها ومنه ﴿تلك حدود الله فلا تقربوها﴾ [البقرة: 187] وحدود الله أيضاً أحكامه لأنها تمنع من التخطي إلى ما وراءها ومنه ﴿تلك حدود الله فلا تعتدوها﴾ ولأن كفارة اليمين دائرة بين العقوبة والعبادة فناسب أن يذكر العقوبات المحضة بعدها. قوله: (الحد عقوبة مقدرة لله تعالى) بيان لمعناه شرعاً فخرج التعزير لعدم التقدير ولا ينافيه قولهم إن أقله ثلاثة وأكثره تسعة وثلاثون سوطاً لأن ما بين الأقل والأكثر ليس بمقدر، ولأنه يكون بغير الضرب وخرج القصاص لأنه حق العبد فلا يسمى حداً على المشهور، وقيل يسمى به فهو العقوبة المقدرة شرعاً فهو على هذا قسمان: قسم يصح فيه العفو وهو القصاص، وقسم لا يصح فيه وهو ما عداه وعلى الأول المشهور الحد لا يقبل مطلقاً بعد ثبوت سببه عند الحاكم وعلى هذا يبنى عدم جواز الشفاعة فيه فإنها إسقاط. قوله: (وقد يقال إن كان الاستثناء الخ) قال في النهر: التحقيق أن الاستثناء راجع إلى عذاب
In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. The Book of Hadd. Hadd linguistically means prevention. From it, the gatekeeper is called haddad because he prevents people from entering, and the jailer is called haddad because he prevents people from leaving. The boundaries of lands are called hudud because they prevent entering someone else's property and part of it going out to another. The comprehensive (jami') that prevents is called hadd because it combines the meaning of a thing and prevents other than it from entering it. Pure punishments are called hudud because they are deterrents from committing their causes again. Allah's hudud are His sacred limits because they are prohibited; from that is {These are the limits of Allah, so do not approach them} [Quran 2:187]. Also, Allah's hudud are His rulings because they prevent transgressing beyond them; from that is {These are the limits of Allah, so do not transgress them}. And because the expiation for an oath revolves between punishment and worship, it was appropriate to mention pure punishments after it. His saying: (Hadd is a fixed punishment prescribed by Allah) is an explanation of its meaning legally. Thus tazir is excluded due to lack of fixation. Their saying that its minimum is three and maximum is thirty-nine lashes does not contradict because what is between the minimum and maximum is not fixed, and because it can be with other than beating. Qisas is excluded because it is the right of the servant; according to the well-known view, it is not called hadd, though some say it is. Thus it (hadd) is legally a fixed punishment. On this basis, it is of two types: a type in which forgiveness is valid, which is qisas, and a type in which it is not valid, which is other than that. According to the first, the well-known view is that hadd is not accepted at all after its cause is established before the judge, and upon this is based the impermissibility of intercession in it, for it is a dropping (of the penalty). His saying: (And it may be said if the exception is...) He said in al-Nahr: The verification is that the exception refers to punishment.
Source pages p. 2

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq begins the Book of Hudud. It first defines 'hadd' linguistically as prevention, giving examples of gatekeepers and jailers, then scriptural usage as limits. Legally, hadd is a fixed punishment prescribed by Allah. The author distinguishes it from tazir (discretionary punishment) which is not fixed, and from qisas (retaliation) which is a private right. He notes that according to the dominant view, qisas is not called hadd. He clarifies that tazir's range of 3-39 lashes does not make it fixed because the specific number is not predetermined. Hadd, once its cause is proven before a judge, cannot be waived or forgiven, unlike qisas where the victim can pardon. This is why intercession (shafa'ah) is not permitted for hadd penalties. The passage ends with a reference to a discussion of an exception in al-Nahr.

Key Terms
الحد (al-hadd): A fixed punishment prescribed by Allah for specific crimes, as defined legally in Hanafi fiqh.
التعزير (al-taʿzīr): A discretionary punishment imposed by the judge for crimes that are not subject to hadd or qisas.
القصاص (al-qisās): Retaliation in kind for intentional homicide or bodily harm; a right of the victim or heirs.
حق الله (ḥaqq Allāh): A right that belongs to Allah, which cannot be waived by individuals after it is established.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the key characteristic that distinguishes hadd from tazir?
A) Hadd is a right of Allah, tazir is a right of the servant
B) Hadd is a fixed punishment, tazir is not fixed
C) Hadd requires four witnesses, tazir requires two
D) Hadd can be forgiven, tazir cannot

Key Takeaway
Hadd is a fixed punishment for a right of Allah that cannot be waived after establishment before a judge, distinguishing it from tazir and qisas.

Hudud — Fixed Penalties
beginner
6 min
The Definition of Hadd: Fixed Punishment for Allah's Right
Defines 'hadd' legally and linguistically, distinguishing from ta'zir and qisas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of hadd and distinguish it from ta'zir and qisas using the criteria of taqdir and haqq al-‘abd.

Core Idea
Hadd is a fixed punishment for Allah's right; it cannot be pardoned after the case reaches the judge.

In this lesson, we unpack the opening of Kitab al-Hudud from the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq and its commentary in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. You will learn the precise legal definition of 'hadd' and why ta'zir (discretionary punishment) and qisas (retaliation) are excluded. The linguistic preamble shows that the concept of prevention (man‘) runs through all meanings of the word. This foundational definition governs the entire book of hudud and has practical consequences for enforcement and intercession.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic root
The word 'hadd' in Arabic conveys prevention (man‘) – the gatekeeper prevents entry, boundaries prevent trespass, and legal definitions prevent incorrect inclusion. Recognize that the legal term builds on this core idea.
This linguistic link helps remember that hudud are meant to deter and prevent crimes.
2
Extract the legal definition
The matn states: 'al-haddu ‘uqubatun muqaddaratun lillahi ta‘ala.' This is a definition by genus and differentia. The genus is 'punishment'; the differentia are 'fixed' (muqaddarah) and 'for Allah ta‘ala' – meaning it is a right of Allah, not a private right.
The word 'muqaddarah' distinguishes hadd from ta'zir; 'lillahi ta‘ala' distinguishes it from qisas.
3
Distinguish from ta'zir
Ta'zir is excluded because it is not fixed (ghayr muqaddar). Even if scholars mention a range (3–39 lashes), the exact amount is not predetermined, and ta'zir can be non‑corporal. Hadd, in contrast, has a precise fixed penalty.
The distinction is based on the presence or absence of taqdir (determination).
4
Distinguish from qisas
Qisas is excluded because it is a right of the slave (haqq al-‘abd) – it belongs to the victim or heirs and can be pardoned. In the mashhur view, qisas is not called hadd. Some scholars do call it hadd, but that would make hadd divisible into pardonable and non‑pardonable types.
The mashhur view is that hadd, once its cause is established before the judge, cannot be dropped by pardon or intercession.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الحدود
الحد عقوبة مقدرة لله تعالى والزنا وطء في قبل خال عن ملك وشبهته ويثبت
كتاب الحدود
في اللغة
لما كانت اليمين للمنع في أحد نوعيها ناسب أن يذكر الحدود عقيبها لأن الحد البواب حداداً لمنعه الناس عن الدخول والسجان حداداً لمنعه عن الخروج، المنع ومنه سمي اللفظ وحدود الديار نهاياتها لمنعها عن دخول ملك الغير فيها وخروج بعضها إليه، وسمي الجامع المانع حداً لأنه يجمع معنى الشيء ويمنع دخول غيره فيه، وسميت العقوبات الخالصة حدوداً لأنها موانع من ارتكاب أسبابها معاودة وحدود الله محارمه لأنها ممنوع عنها ومنه تلك حدود الله فلا تقربوها ﴾ [البقرة : ۱۸۷] وحدود الله أيضاً أحكامه لأنها تمنع من التخطي إلى ما وراءها ومنه حدود الله فلا تعتدوها ولأن كفارة اليمين دائرة بين العقوبة والعبادة فناسب أن يذكر العقوبات المحضة بعدها.
قوله : (الحد عقوبة مقدرة تعالى) بيان لمعناه شرعاً فخرج التعزير لعدم التقدير ولا ينافيه قولهم إن أقله ثلاثة وأكثره تسعة وثلاثون سوطاً لأن ما بين الأقل والأكثر ليس بمقدر، ولأنه يكون بغير الضرب وخرج القصاص لأنه حق العبد فلا حداً اصطلاحاً على يسمى المشهور، وقيل يسمى به فهو العقوبة المقدرة شرعاً فهو على هذا قسمان: قسم يصح فيه العفو وهو القصاص، وقسم لا يصح فيه وهو ما عداه وعلى الأول المشهور الحد لا يقبل مطلقاً بعد ثبوت سببه عند الحاكم وعلى هذا يبنى عدم جواز الشفاعة فيها فإنها الإسقاط
Book of Hudud. The hadd is a punishment determined by Allah Almighty. Zina is intercourse in the front [vagina] that is free from ownership and its semblance. It is established. Book of Hudud. In language: Since the oath (yamin) is for prevention in one of its two types, it is appropriate to mention hudud after it, because the gatekeeper (haddab) is called haddad because he prevents people from entering, and the jailer (sajjan) is called haddad because he prevents [prisoners] from exiting. Prevention (man') and from it the word is named. The boundaries of houses (hudud al-diyar) are their end limits because they prevent entry into another's property and exit from it to another. The comprehensive definition (al-jami' al-mani') is called hadd because it encompasses the meaning of a thing and prevents inclusion of other than it. The pure punishments (al-uqubat al-khalisah) are called hudud because they are preventives from committing their causes and from repetition. Allah's hudud are His prohibitions because they are prohibited; from it is {These are the limits of Allah, so do not approach them} [al-Baqarah 2:187]. Allah's hudud are also His rulings because they prevent transgression beyond them; from it is {These are the limits of Allah, so do not transgress them} [al-Baqarah 2:229]. And because the expiation of an oath (kaffarat al-yamin) oscillates between punishment and worship, it was appropriate to mention the purely punitive [punishments] after it. His statement: (The hadd is a punishment determined by Allah Almighty) is an explanation of its meaning legally. Thus ta'zir is excluded due to lack of determination (taqdir). And it is not contradicted by their statement that its minimum is three and its maximum is thirty-nine lashes, because what is between the minimum and maximum is not determined, and because it can be other than beating. And qisas is excluded because it is a right of the slave (haqq al-‘abd), so it is not called hadd by convention in the well-known [view]; and it is said it is called so. So it is the punishment determined legally. Thus it is of two types: a type where pardon is valid, which is qisas, and a type where pardon is not valid, which is other than it. And according to the first [well-known view], hadd is absolutely not accepted after its cause is established with the judge, and upon this is built the impermissibility of intercession (shafa'ah) in it, for it is nullification (isqat).
Source pages p. 3

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification ruling. It begins with the title 'Kitab al-Hudud' and gives a linguistic preamble explaining why the book on hudud follows the book on oaths: because oaths involve prevention, and hudud are also preventive. The word 'hadd' in Arabic has many related meanings: a gatekeeper (haddab), the boundary of a house, and a legal definition (al-jami' al-mani'). All share the idea of prevention (man'). The author then quotes the matn: 'Al-haddu 'uqubatun muqaddaratun lillahi ta'ala'—the hadd is a fixed punishment determined by Allah. The commentator explains that this definition excludes ta'zir (discretionary punishment) because ta'zir is not fixed; its range is not determined. Even if scholars say its minimum is three and maximum thirty-nine lashes, the specific number is not fixed, and ta'zir can be non-corporal. Qisas (retaliation) is also excluded because it is a right of the victim (haqq al-'abd); it is not called hadd in the well-known view (mashhur), though some call it hadd. If we accept that qisas is a hadd, then hadd is of two types: one where pardon is valid (qisas) and one where it is not (all others). According to the mashhur view, once the cause of a hadd is established before the judge, the hadd cannot be dropped by pardon, and intercession is impermissible because it would nullify the prescribed punishment. This all belongs to the introduction of the book on hudud.

Key Terms
الحد (al-hadd): A fixed punishment prescribed by Allah for specific offenses, determined in amount and manner.
التعزير (al-ta'zir): Discretionary punishment not fixed in amount, can be less than hadd or non-corporal.
القصاص (al-qisas): Retaliation for killing or injury; a right of the victim, pardonable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi definition in this passage, what distinguishes hadd from ta'zir?
A) Hadd is a punishment for Allah's right while ta'zir is for private rights
B) Hadd is fixed in amount while ta'zir is not
C) Hadd must be carried out immediately while ta'zir can be delayed
D) Hadd is only for zina while ta'zir is for all other crimes

Key Takeaway
Hadd is a fixed punishment for Allah's right; it cannot be pardoned once proven before the judge.

Hudud — Fixed Penalties
intermediate
12 min
The Four Hudud and the Hanafi Position on Expiation of Sin
Classification of hudud and debate on expiation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four hudud and their purposes, and explain why the Hanafi school holds that hadd punishment does not automatically expiate sin without repentance.

Core Idea
Hudud are preventive and deterrent; their enforcement alone does not erase the sin—repentance is required.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq, the author clarifies the classification of hudud and enters a key debate: does the fixed punishment itself purify the sinner in the Hereafter? The Hanafi school argues it does not, relying on a definitive Quranic verse. This lesson unpacks the categories of hudud and the proof structure behind the Hanafi position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Four Hudud and Their Rationales
Read the portion where the author lists the four hudud: zina (protects lineage), sariqa (protects wealth), shurb (protects intellect), and qadhf (protects honor). Note that some scholars count five, but the Hanafi view consolidates intoxication under drinking.
The text also mentions that hudud are preventive before the act and deterrent after.
2
State the Central Question
The debate: Does the hadd punishment itself serve as expiation (kaffarah) for the sin in the Hereafter, or is repentance (tawbah) necessary? Recognize that many scholars hold the hadd alone expiates, while the Hanafi school disagrees.
3
Present the Hanafi Proof from the Quran
The Hanafi rely on Surah al-Ma'idah (5:34) which combines worldly disgrace and Hereafter punishment for highway robbers, with an exception for those who repent. The exception (illa) only removes the Hereafter punishment, not the worldly hadd, proving that punishment alone does not remove sin.
This is a qat'i (definitive) evidence.
4
Analyze the Opposing Proof and Hanafi Response
The opponent cites a hadith: 'Whoever is punished for a sin in this world, it is an expiation for him.' The Hanafi response: this hadith is zanni (conjectural) and must be interpreted (ta'wil) to harmonize with the Quran. The apparent meaning is that punishment leads to repentance, so the expiation is conditional on the repentance that the punishment typically induces.
The principle: zanni evidence must yield to qat'i when they conflict.
5
Determine the Practical Consequence (Thamarah al-Khilaf)
If a hadd is carried out without the offender repenting, Hanafis hold that the sin remains in the Hereafter; other schools consider the sin erased. This affects how judges and muftis advise criminals and whether they encourage repentance.

Source Text

That is why the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) objected to Usamah ibn Zayd when he interceded for the Makhzumiyyah woman who stole, saying: Do you intercede in a hadd from the hudud of Allah? And before reaching the Imam and its establishment with him, intercession is permitted for the one raising [the case] to the judge to let him go, because the hadd has not been established; thus in Fath. And the verification is that hudud are preventives before the act and deterrents after it, i.e., knowledge of their legality prevents committing the act, and their infliction afterwards prevents returning to it. So they are from the rights of Allah Almighty, because they were legislated for a benefit that reverts to all people. Thus their original ruling is deterrence from what harms the servants and protection of the Abode of Islam from... For in the hadd of zina is protection of lineages, in the hadd of theft is protection of wealth, in the hadd of drinking is protection of intellects, and in the hadd of slander is protection of honors. Thus the hudud are four. And what is in al-Bada'i' that they are five, making the fifth the hadd of intoxication (sukr), is unnecessary, because the hadd of intoxication is the hadd of drinking in quantity and quality, even if the cause differs. And the scholars differed on whether purification from sin is among its rulings without repentance. Many scholars went to that, and our companions (Ashabuna) went to that it is not among its rulings. So if the hadd is inflicted on him and he does not repent, the sin of that transgression is not removed from him according to us, acting upon the verse of the highway robbers: He said Almighty: {That is for them a disgrace in this world, and for them in the Hereafter is a great punishment, except those who repent} [al-Ma'idah 5:34]. For the demonstrative pronoun refers to killing or crucifixion or exile. Thus Allah Almighty combined the punishment of this world and the Hereafter upon them, and removed the punishment of the Hereafter through repentance, because the exception refers to it, by consensus that repentance does not remove the hadd in this world. As for what Bukhari and others narrated as marfu': "Whoever commits something of these sins and is punished for it in this world, it is an expiation for him; and whoever commits something of them and Allah conceals him, then it is up to Allah: if He wills He forgives him, and if He wills He punishes him" (1), it must be interpreted as when he repents at the punishment, because apparent is that his beating or stoning comes with repentance from him due to his tasting the cause of his action. So [the hadith] is restricted by that to combine [the evidences]. And restricting the zanni (conjectural) when opposed by the qati' (definitive) is necessary, contrary to the opposite; thus in Fath al-Qadir. And it may be said: If the exception in the verse refers to the punishment of the Hereafter, there remains no [problem] for His statement "from before that you are able" (min qabl an taqdiru) regarding the punishment of this world and the Hereafter, so that if he dies before being seized after having terrorized the road and did not kill or take anything, the hadd of this world and the punishment of the Hereafter drop from him. However, if he terrorized the road and repented after taking, the hadd of this world does not drop from him, as will come. And by this appears the benefit of the restriction with "before being able." And the statement of the commentator that the exception refers to what precedes it from the sentences due to the unity of their kind, so the whole is removed by repentance, and he returned to what follows in the verse of qadhf due to its difference from what precedes it, so it was a separator. End. And he intends by removal of the whole the aggregate, because you know that if he killed or took property and repented, it does not drop from him. (1) Recorded by Bukhari in Kitab al-Iman, Bab 11; Kitab al-Hudud, Bab 8; Muslim in Kitab al-Hudud, Hadith 41; Tirmidhi in Kitab al-Hudud, Bab 12; Nasa'i in Kitab al-Bay'ah, Bab 9; Ahmad in his Musnad (5/... The apparent that he is the evidence.
Source pages p. 3

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof, specifically an expansion of the text from Kanz al-Daqaiq. It begins by quoting a gloss from al-Nahr regarding an exception (istithna) in the verse of highway robbers. Then it returns to the main commentary on hudud. The author clarifies that intercession (shafa'ah) before the hadd is established with the ruler is permissible, as the hadd has not become obligatory. Once established, it is impermissible. Then the purpose of hudud is explained: they are preventive before the act and deterrent after. Hudud are four: zina (protects lineage), sariqa (protects wealth), shurb (protects intellect), and qadhf (protects honor). The author notes that al-Bada'i' mentions five including hadd of intoxication, but that is the same as hadd of shurb. The core debate: do the hudud purify the sinner from sin without repentance? The Hanafi school (Ashabuna) holds that they do not; the punishment does not automatically expiate sin. They rely on the verse of highway robbers (al-Ma'idah 5:34) which combines disgrace in this world and punishment in the Hereafter, except for those who repent. The exception (illa) only removes the Hereafter punishment, not the worldly hadd, by consensus. The hadith that punishment is an expiation is interpreted as applying when the criminal also repents, as the painful experience of punishment typically leads to repentance. The argument relies on prioritizing qat'i (definitive) over zanni (conjectural) evidence. The passage then discusses subtle points of the verse regarding timing of repentance and its effect on the hadd. This entire discussion belongs to the Hanafi position on the legal effect of hudud.

Key Terms
الحدود (al-hudud): Fixed punishments prescribed by Allah for specific crimes: zina, qadhf, shurb, sariqa.
الكفارة (al-kaffarah): Expiation; here, whether the hadd punishment itself serves as expiation for the sin in the Hereafter.
الاستثناء (al-istithna): The exception clause (illa) in the verse; key to the dispute.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is required for the hadd punishment to expiate the sin of the offender in the Hereafter?
A) The punishment itself is sufficient.
B) The offender must also repent (tawbah) during or before the punishment.
C) The offender must pay compensation to the victim.
D) The offender must perform additional good deeds.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that hudud do not expiate sin without repentance, based on the Quranic verse (5:34) and the principle of preferring qat'i over zanni evidence.

Does Hadd Punishment Expel Sin? Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Inter-madhhab dispute on expiatory effect of hudud

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on whether hadd punishment expiates sin and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument by analyzing the proofs.

Core Idea
The hadd does not automatically purify from sin; repentance is required for forgiveness in the Hereafter.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a fundamental question: does the worldly punishment (hadd) for major sins also cleanse the sinner spiritually? The Hanafi position is that the hadd and the sin are distinct. The punishment is a worldly right of Allah, but the sin's spiritual guilt remains unless the person repents. This lesson unpacks the evidence and the dispute with the Shafi'i school.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
The Hanafi school holds that the hadd punishment does not expiate the sin. The sin remains unless the person repents to Allah.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Many scholars, including the Shafi'i school, believe that the hadd itself expiates the sin, citing a hadith that punishment in this world is expiation.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The hadith: 'Whoever commits any of these sins and is punished for it in this world, it is an expiation for him.' This seems to imply automatic purification.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response is that the hadith must be interpreted as referring to those who repent during the punishment. The definitive Quranic verse (5:34) is given precedence, which shows that repentance removes Hereafter punishment, not the hadd.
The principle: qati' (definitive) overrides zanni (speculative) evidence.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: If a person is punished but does not repent, the Hanafi holds that sin remains and he may be punished in the Hereafter. The opponent holds that the sin is completely expiated by the punishment alone.
This affects the mufti's advice and the criminal's own religious obligation.

A benefit: because repentance removes sin before and after capture and ability over them. So apparently it refers back to worldly punishment because it will come that the hadd of highway robbers drops by repentance before ability over them, and only the rights of servants remain: retaliation if they killed, and amputation if they took property, so their pardon is valid, unlike after ability, for it does not drop the right of Allah, so that the pardon of the heirs of the murdered is not valid. And al-Zayla'i argued that it (hadd) is not purifying from sin because it is established against the disbeliever and it is not purifying by consensus. And some added that it is established against his will on whom it is established. The second is nothing because of the permissibility of expiation by the hardships that befall a person even if he is not patient, as Imam al-Shafi'i stated. The summary: the obligatory upon the disobedient person in reality is repentance between himself and Allah, and turning back. Then when its proof reaches the imam, establishing the hadd becomes obligatory upon the imam, and he does not refrain from establishing it due to repentance. In al-Dhahiriyya: a man committed an indecency then repented and turned to Allah, he should not inform the judge of his indecency to establish the hadd upon him, because concealment is recommended.
Source pages p. 4

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute about whether the hadd punishment purifies the sinner from sin. The author begins by defining hudud as deterrents: they prevent commission of sins and discourage repetition. They are rights of Allah for public benefit. Hudud are four: for zina, theft, drinking, and slander. Then the dispute is presented: many scholars (including Shafi'is) believe that the hadd itself expiates sin. The Hanafi school (our companions) holds that it does not; even if the hadd is applied, the sin remains unless the person repents. The evidence is the verse about highway robbers (5:34), which combines disgrace in this world and punishment in the Hereafter, except for those who repent. The repentance removes the Hereafter punishment but does not affect the worldly hadd. The hadith that punishment is expiation is interpreted as when the person repents during punishment. The commentator reconciles the evidences, giving priority to the definitive Qur'anic text. A benefit is discussed: for highway robbers, repentance before capture drops the hadd but not the rights of servants (like retaliation or compensation). After capture, the hadd is not dropped. The passage also notes that the hadd is established even on a disbeliever, which shows it is not purifying. The practical ruling: the sinner must repent privately; if the judge learns of the crime, he must enforce the hadd, but he should not proactively investigate. The Dhahiriyya states that if someone repents, he should not inform the judge.

Key Terms
الحد (al-hadd): A fixed punishment ordained by Allah for specific crimes, a right of Allah
التوبة (al-tawbah): Repentance from sin, which must be sincere and involves abandoning the sin, regret, and resolve not to return
الاستثناء (al-istithna'): The exception clause in a Quranic verse or legal statement that excludes a specific group from the ruling

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a person commits zina and is stoned for it but does not repent in his heart, what is his status?
A) He is purified from the sin and goes to Paradise without punishment
B) The sin is still upon him and he may be punished in the Hereafter
C) The hadd is invalid because he did not repent
D) He must repeat the hadd

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that hadd punishment does not expiate sin; repentance is necessary for Hereafter forgiveness.

The Legal Definition of Zina (Adultery)
Defining zina linguistically and legally with exclusions and inclusions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi legal definition of zina, list its exclusions and inclusions, and explain why the woman is considered a fornicator in reality.

Core Idea
Zina is defined as vaginal intercourse without ownership or its semblance; the woman is a real fornicator by enabling.

This lesson unpacks the precise Hanafi definition of zina from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. We will dissect its key components—intercourse, front passage, absence of ownership—and explore the important inclusion of the woman's enabling act. By the end, you will see why the Hanafi school treats the woman as a real fornicator, not a metaphorical one, and how this definition applies to various scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Parse the definition phrase
Identify that zina is defined as 'wat' fī qabl khālī 'an al-milk wa shubhatihi'—intercourse in the front passage devoid of ownership and its semblance.
2
List the express exclusions
The definition explicitly excludes anal intercourse (dubur), intercourse with one's wife/slave-woman, and cases with a semblance of ownership (shubha).
3
Examine the inclusion of the father-son slave-girl case
Understand why this act is legally zina despite the hadd being waived, evidenced by the slanderer not facing the qadhf hadd.
This shows that the definition stands independently of the penalty.
4
Resolve the objection about the woman
The initial answer that the woman is a fornicator metaphorically is refined: she is real because Allah calls her zāniyah and she is subject to hadd. The definition includes tamkīn to cover her act.
5
Note the significance of tamkīn
Tamkīn (enabling) is included so that the woman's action falls under the defined term, making her a real fornicator just like the man.
The commentary refutes the earlier metaphorical view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والزنا وطء في قبل حال عن الملك وشبهته بيان لمعناه الشرعي واللغوي فإنهما سواء فيه. وخرج الوطء في الدبر، وخرج وطء زوجته وأمته ومن له فيها شبهة ملك، ودخل وطء الأب جارية ابنه فإنه زنا شرعي بدليل أنه لا يحد قاذفه بالزنا وإن لم يجب الحد عليه والمراد وطء الرجل فخرج الصبي لكن يرد عليه المرأة فإن فعلها ليس وطئاً، وإنما هو تمكين منه ، والجواب أن تسميتها زانية مجاز والكلام في الحقيقة. ولم يقصد المصنف تعريف واحد منهما، سواء تاب قبل الأخذ أو بعده .اهـ. قلت : وفي حمله الكل على المجموع نظر ظاهر لأن الكلام في سقوط الحد ولا شبهة في سقوطه فيما لو قتل أو أخذ المال ثم تاب قبل القدرة عليه، وإنما لا يسقط القتل والضمان لكونه حق عبد حتى لو عفا عنه صح كما يأتي. قوله : (والقطع أن أخذوا المال) صوابه والضمان بدل قوله والقطع». وعبارته في باب قطاع الطريق الثانية : لو قتل فتاب قبل الأخذ لا حد لأن هذه الجناية لا تقام بعد التوبة للاستثناء المذكور في النص أو لأن التوبة تتوقف على رد المال ولا قطع في مثله فظهر حق العبد في النفس والمال حتى يستوفي الولي القصاص أو يعفو، ويجب الضمان إذا هلك في يده أو استهلكه؛ كذا في الهداية .اهـ. قوله : (والجواب أن تسميتها زانية مجاز والكلام في (الحقيقة اعلم أنه لما كانت المرأة تحد حد الزنا وقد سماها الله تعالى زانية في قوله تعالى الزانية والزاني علم أنها تسمى زانية حقيقة ولا يلزم من كونها لا تسمى واطئة أنها زانية مجازاً فلذا زاد في التعريف تمكينها حتى يدخل فعلها في المعرف وهو الزنا الموجب للحد، فلو لم يكن تمكينها زناً حقيقة لما احتيج إلى إدخاله في التعريف وهو أيضاً أمارة كونها زانية حقيقة وإن لم تكن واطئة كما أن الرجل يسمى زانياً حقيقة بالتمكين وإن لم يوجد منه الوطء حقيقة، و به سقط ما في البحر من أن تسميتها زانية مجاز فافهم اهـ. يقول الفقير أحمد جامع . هذه الحواشي :
His saying: (And zina is intercourse in the front passage without ownership or its semblance) is an explanation of its legal and linguistic meaning, for they are the same in it. And it excludes anal intercourse, and it excludes intercourse with one's wife, his slave-woman, and the one with whom he has a semblance of ownership. And it includes the intercourse of a father with his son's slave-girl, for it is legally zina, evidenced by the fact that the one who slanders him (the father) is not punished with the hadd of slander, even though the hadd (for zina) is not obligatory upon him. And the intent is the intercourse of a man, so it excludes a boy. But it is objected concerning the woman: her action is not intercourse; it is only enabling. And the answer is that calling her a fornicator is metaphorical, and the discussion is in reality. And the author (al-Musannif) did not intend to define one of them, whether he repented before or after being caught. ... He said: (And the amputation if they took property) the correct is 'and the guarantee' instead of his word 'and the amputation'. And his expression in the chapter of highway robbers second: if he killed then repented before being caught, there is no hadd because this crime is not established after repentance due to the exception mentioned in the text, or because repentance depends on returning the property, and there is no amputation in its like, so the right of the servant in life and property appears, so that the guardian can execute retaliation or pardon, and the guarantee becomes obligatory if it perished in his hand or he consumed it; so in al-Hidaya. His saying: (And the answer that calling her a fornicator is metaphorical and the discussion is in reality. Know that since the woman is punished with the hadd of zina and Allah called her a fornicator in His saying 'The fornicator and the fornicator,' it is known that she is called a fornicator in reality. It does not follow from her not being called an intercourse-performer that she is a fornicator metaphorically. Hence the author added 'enabling' in the definition so that her action enters into the defined, which is zina that necessitates the hadd. For if her enabling were not zina in reality, there would be no need to include it in the definition. And it is also an indication that she is a fornicator in reality even if she is not an intercourse-performer, just as a man is called a fornicator in reality by enabling even if actual intercourse does not occur from him. And with this, what is in al-Bahr that calling her a fornicator is metaphorical is refuted. So understand. Says the poor Ahmad, compiler of these marginal notes:
Source pages p. 5

Commentary
This passage provides a definition of zina (adultery) as a legal term. The definition is: 'intercourse in the front passage without ownership or its semblance.' The author explains that the legal and linguistic meanings are the same here. The definition excludes anal intercourse (liwāt), intercourse with one's wife, slave-woman, or a woman with whom there is a semblance of ownership (shubha). It includes the case of a father having intercourse with his son's slave-girl because this is legally zina, even though the father is not subject to the hadd penalty; the evidence is that if someone slanders the father for this act, the slanderer is not punished with the hadd of qadhf (false accusation of zina), showing that the act is indeed zina but the penalty is waived for other reasons. The definition also specifies that the subject is a man (mukallaf), so a minor boy's act is excluded. However, a question arises regarding the woman: her act is not intercourse but merely enabling (tamkīn). The initial answer given is that she is called a fornicator metaphorically, not literally. The commentator then refines this: since Allah calls women 'zāniyah' (fornicatress) in the Quran, and since she is subject to the hadd, she is called a fornicator in reality. The author of the definition accordingly included 'enabling' (tamkīn) in the definition to encompass her action. Thus, a woman who enables intercourse is a real fornicator, just as a man who enables (i.e., initiates) is a real fornicator even if actual penetration does not occur. The passage also includes a digression (likely a marginal note) about highway robbery, correcting a textual phrase from 'amputation' to 'guarantee' (daman), which is a separate ruling.

Key Terms
الوطء (al-wat'): Sexual intercourse involving penetration of the glans (or its equivalent) into the vagina
شبهة الملك (shubhat al-milk): A semblance of ownership that makes the sexual act lawful, such as a mistaken belief that one owns a woman
التمكين (al-tamkīn): The woman's enabling of intercourse, considered as the equivalent of the man's act for the purpose of defining zina

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi definition include the woman's 'tamkīn' (enabling) as part of the definition of zina?
A) To ensure the man is punished
B) Because the woman's act is not intercourse but enabling, and without inclusion she would escape the definition
C) Because it is required for the hadd to be applied
D) Because the author preferred it

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of zina precisely defines the act and includes the woman's enabling, establishing her as a real fornicator subject to hadd.

Repentance of Highway Robbers and Its Effect on Hadd
Explaining when repentance drops the hadd for highway robbers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on when repentance drops the hadd for highway robbery and explain the distinction between rights of Allah and rights of humans.

Core Idea
Repentance before capture drops the hadd of hirabah; after capture, the hadd remains wajib as a right of Allah.

The question of whether repentance can avert a fixed punishment (hadd) is a classic legal puzzle. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, commenting on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, tackles the specific case of highway robbers (quttāʿ al-ṭarīq). The ruling hinges on a crisp temporal distinction: repentance before the authorities gain power drops the worldly punishment, but rights of victims remain; repentance after capture does not affect the hadd. The text also engages with the broader debate about whether hadd is purifying of sin.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
The passage addresses whether and when the hadd punishment for hirabah (highway robbery) is dropped by the offender's repentance (tawbah). Focus on the timing relative to capture.
The key words: 'قبل القدرة' (before having power) and 'بعدها' (after it).
2
Extract the ruling from the Quranic proof
The author cites Quran 5:34, interpreting 'except those who repent before you overpower them' to indicate that repentance before capture drops the worldly punishment. Understand this as a dalil of dalala zanni.
The verse is cited in the form 'عليهم' which refers to the end of the verse.
3
Distinguish between rights of Allah and rights of humans
Even if the hadd (right of Allah) is dropped by pre-capture repentance, the rights of victims (rights of humans) remain: qisas if murder, and restoration of property. Pardon is valid only from the victims.
This is the Hanafi position: huquq Allah can be waived by tawbah before capture, but huquq al-ibad are not extinguished.
4
Analyze the effect of post-capture repentance
Repentance after capture does not drop the hadd. The text explains that the hadd is a right of Allah that must be enforced once the case reaches the Imam. It also refutes the idea that hadd purifies from sin by noting it is applied to disbelievers.
This contrasts with al-Shafi'i's view that hadd is expiatory. Hanafis hold it is not necessarily purifying.
5
Apply the practical advice from al-Zahiriyyah
If an offender repents secretly before being reported, he should not inform the judge to have the hadd carried out, because concealment is recommended (mandub). This is an application of the principle that once the matter is concealed, it is better left.
This shows the subtlety: the ruler's duty to establish hadd is conditional on knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الحدود عليهم [المائدة : ٣٤] فائدة لأن التوبة ترفع الذنب قبل الأخذ والقدرة عليهم وبعدها، فالظاهر أنه راجع إلى عذاب الدنيا لما سيأتي أن حد قطاع الطريق يسقط بالتوبة قبل القدرة عليهم وإنما يبقى حق العباد عليهم من القصاص إن قتلوا والقطع إن أخذوا المال فصح العفو عنهم بخلافها بعد القدرة فإنها لا تسقط حق الله تعالى حتى لا يصح عفو أولياء المقتولين. واستدل الزيلعي على عدم كونه مطهراً من الذنب بأنه يقام على الكافر ولا مطهر له اتفاقاً، وزاد بعضهم ويقام على كره ممن أقيم عليه الحد. والثاني ليس بشيء لجواز التكفير بما يصيب الإنسان من المكاره وإن لم يصبر كما نص عليه الإمام الشافعي والحاصل أن الواجب على العاصي في نفس الأمر التوبة فيما بينه وبين الله تعالى والإنابة، ثم إذا اتصل بالإمام ثبوته وجب إقامة الحد على الإمام ولا يمتنع من إقامته بسبب التوبة. وفي الظهيرية : رجل أتى بفاحشة ثم تاب وأناب إلى الله تعالى فإنه لا يعلم القاضي بفاحشته لإقامة الحد عليه لأن الستر مندوب إليه ا هـ .
The Book of Hudud. 'Upon them' (Quran 5:34) is a benefit because repentance removes sin before capture and after it, but the apparent meaning is that it refers to worldly punishment because, as will come, the hadd of highway robbers is dropped by repentance before having power over them, but the rights of people remain: retaliation if they killed, and [cutting] if they took property, so pardon is valid from them. Unlike after having power over them, then it does not drop the right of Allah, so pardon of the guardians of the killed is not valid. Al-Zayla'i argued that it is not purifying from sin because it is carried out on the disbeliever and there is no purifier for him by consensus. Some added that it is carried out unwillingly on whom the hadd is established. The second is nothing because expiation is possible through the hardships that befall a person even if he does not endure, as Imam al-Shafi'i stated. The conclusion is that the obligatory thing for the sinner in reality is repentance between him and Allah, and turning back. Then if his guilt reaches the Imam, establishing hadd becomes obligatory upon the Imam, and he is not prevented from establishing it because of repentance. In al-Zahiriyyah: A man committed an indecency then repented and turned back to Allah; then he should not inform the judge of his indecency to establish hadd upon him, because concealment is recommended.
Source pages p. 5

Commentary
This passage discusses the effect of repentance on the hadd punishment for highway robbers (qat' al-tariq). It begins by citing the Quranic verse (5:34) which is understood to refer to worldly punishment, not the afterlife. The key distinction is timing: repentance before the authorities have power over the robber drops the hadd penalty for the crime of hirabah (highway robbery), but it does not extinguish the rights of the victims (huquq al-ibad). Thus, if murder was committed, the victim's heirs still have the right to qisas (retaliation) or pardon. Similarly, if property was taken, the obligation to return it or compensate remains. Repentance after capture does not drop the hadd because it is a right of Allah that must be enforced; the example of the disbeliever upon whom hadd is established shows it is not purifying. The passage concludes with a fatwa from al-Zahiriyyah that if someone secretly repents, he should not inform the judge to have the hadd carried out, as concealment is recommended (mandub).

Key Terms
توبة (tawbah): Repentance that is sincere and involves turning away from the sin; in this context, it affects the hadd ruling depending on timing.
حد (hadd): A fixed punishment prescribed by Allah for specific crimes, considered a right of Allah.
قطاع الطريق (quṭṭāʿ al-ṭarīq): Highway robbers or bandits who ambush travelers; the crime is hirabah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position, what is the effect of repentance after the authorities have captured a highway robber?
A) The hadd is dropped entirely
B) The hadd is not dropped, but the victims' rights are also extinguished
C) The hadd is not dropped, but the victims' rights remain
D) The hadd is dropped if the robber returns the property

Key Takeaway
The timing of repentance relative to capture determines whether the hadd for highway robbery is dropped; pre-capture repentance drops the hadd, post-capture does not, but victims' rights always remain.

Hanafi Definition of Zina That Obligates Hadd
Definition and conditions of zina for hadd

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of zina that obligates hadd and list its conditions, including the condition of knowledge.

Core Idea
The hadd for zina requires a legally responsible, willing person, with a desired female, in dar al-Islam, and knowledge of the prohibition.

The passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq defines zina for the purposes of the hadd punishment. While the matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq defines zina generally as vaginal intercourse free from ownership, the commentary clarifies that for the hadd to apply, additional conditions must be met. These conditions ensure that the punishment is only imposed when there is no semblance of excuse, reflecting the Hanafi commitment to caution in applying penalties.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base definition from the matn
The matn defines zina as intercourse in the front while free from ownership and its semblance. This is the shar'i definition, encompassing all prohibited intercourse.
This includes cases like anal intercourse and intercourse with a corpse, which are zina in the linguistic/legal sense but do not incur hadd.
2
Critique al-Zayla'i's narrow definition
Al-Zayla'i defined the zina that obligates hadd as intercourse of a mukallaf in the front of a desired female free from ownership, willingly. The passage shows this is invalid in inclusion (excludes cases like madness) and exclusion (excludes woman's enabling).
The text argues that the definition must be for shar'i zina, and conditions like mukallaf are external.
3
Extract the Hanafi definition of zina that obligates hadd
The correct definition: intercourse of a legally responsible (mukallaf), willing (tai') person with a desired female (mushtahat), in the front (qabl), without any semblance of ownership (shubhat milk), in dar al-Islam, or his enabling (tamkin) or her enabling. Additionally, knowledge of the prohibition is a condition.
This definition includes the woman's enabling as actual zina, solving the earlier objection.
4
Understand the condition of knowledge
The hadd requires that the person knew zina is forbidden. This is based on the hadith of 'Umar: 'If he knows that Allah has forbidden zina, then flog him; if he does not know, teach him...' Ignorance creates a shubha (doubt) that prevents the hadd.
Even widespread notoriety (shi'ya) in dar al-Islam does not replace actual knowledge for the hadd, though it may for other rulings.
5
Recognize the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
The definition for hadd is narrower than shar'i zina. Cases like anal intercourse or intercourse with a corpse are shar'i zina but do not attract hadd. The Hanafi position is that the matn defines shar'i zina, and the conditions for hadd are external.
This affects whether conditions like dar al-Islam are part of the essence or external - the Hanafi view is external.

His saying: (And zina is intercourse in the front while being free from ownership and its semblance) is an explanation of its legal and linguistic meaning, for they are the same in it. Excluded is intercourse in the anus, and excluded is intercourse with his wife, his slave woman, and someone in whom he has a semblance of ownership. Included is a father's intercourse with his son's slave girl, for it is shar'i zina evidenced by the fact that one who accuses him of zina is not given the hadd, even though the hadd is not obligatory on him. The intended meaning is the man's intercourse, so the minor is excluded, but the woman is problematic because her act is not intercourse, rather it is enabling. The answer is that calling her a fornicator is metaphorical, and the discussion is about reality. The author did not intend to define either of them, whether he repented before capture or after. I said: His interpretation of the whole as a collection is apparently problematic because the discussion is about the dropping of hadd, and there is no doubt it drops if he killed or took property then repented before having power over him. Only execution and guarantee do not drop because it is a right of a person, so if he pardons, it is valid. His saying: (And the cutting is that they took the property) - the correct is 'and the guarantee' instead of 'and the cutting.' And his expression in the second chapter of highway robbers: If he killed then repented before capture, no hadd because this crime is not carried out after repentance due to the mentioned exception in the text, or because repentance depends on returning the property and there is no cutting in such a case. So the person's right in life and property appears, so the guardian may execute qisas or pardon, and guarantee is obligatory if it perished in his hand or he consumed it; as in al-Hidayah. His saying: (And the answer that calling her a fornicator is metaphorical and the discussion is about reality) - Know that when the woman receives the hadd of zina and Allah called her a fornicator in His saying 'the fornicatress and the fornicator,' it is known that she is called a fornicator in reality, and it does not follow from her not being called one who has intercourse that she is a fornicator metaphorically. Therefore he added 'her enabling' in the definition so that her action enters the definition, which is the zina that obligates the hadd. If her enabling were not actually zina, there would be no need to include it in the definition, and it is also a sign that she is actually a fornicator even if she does not have intercourse, just as a man is actually called a fornicator by enabling even if actual intercourse does not occur from him. And by this, what is in al-Bahr that her being called a fornicator is metaphorical falls down, so understand. The poor Ahmad, compiler of these marginal notes, says: The Book of Hudud. From the insertion of the glans, the zina that obligates the hadd, as al-Zayla'i imagined, for if it were so, the definition would be invalid both in inclusion and exclusion. As for invalidity in inclusion, it exists in the insane, the coerced, in intercourse with a girl who is not desired, a corpse, an animal, and in dar al-harb, and the hadd is not obligatory in these instances, and it is shar'i zina. As for invalidity in exclusion, it is in the woman's zina, for the hadd is absent but the defined thing (the zina that obligates the hadd) is not absent. So the zina that obligates the hadd is the intercourse of a legally responsible, willing person with a desired female, currently or previously, in the front, without any semblance of ownership, in dar al-Islam, or his enabling that or her enabling, so that it applies to the case where he is lying on his back and she sits on his penis and leaves him, then [؟] both are given the hadd in this situation, even though only enabling is present from him. And intercourse is the insertion of a part of the penis into the front or anus. By this it is known that al-Zayla'i's definition of the zina that obligates the hadd as 'intercourse of a legally responsible person in the front of a desired female, free from his ownership and its semblance, willingly' is not complete, even if he claimed it is more complete, as is obvious. Al-Muhit added that among its conditions is knowledge of prohibition, so that if he did not know of the prohibition, the hadd is not obligatory due to shubha. Its basis is what Sa'id ibn al-Musayyab narrated: A man committed zina in Yemen, and 'Umar (RA) wrote concerning him: If he knows that Allah has forbidden zina, then flog him; if he does not know, teach him, and if he returns, flog him. And because rulings in religious matters are not established except after knowledge; if (the crime) is widespread and notorious in dar al-Islam, it stands in place of knowledge, but at least it creates a shubha due to lack of communication. By this it is known that being in dar al-Islam does not stand in place of knowledge for the obligation of hadd, even though it stands in its place for all other rulings. And he responded to it in Fath al-Qadir that zina is forbidden in all religions and nations, so the harbi if... This statement I have not seen in my teacher's handwriting on the margin of al-Bahr here; rather, he mentioned it in his gloss on al-Durr al-Mukhtar, so let it be memorized, may Allah have mercy on him over the ages for his unique research in every place. His saying: (And the author did not intend to define the zina that obligates the hadd, etc.) He said in al-Nahr: You are aware that the invalidity of inclusion is only complete on the assumption that the definition is for the zina that obligates the hadd, but we do not accept that; rather, it is for shar'i zina. And the woman's zina does not invalidate in exclusion because it is not actually zina. And it is not hidden that his enabling invalidates in exclusion even if shar'i is intended, unless by intercourse is meant the insertion of the glans into the front of a desired female. The truth is that this definition is for the zina that obligates the hadd, and those additional conditions are external to the essence, and similar has preceded. Then I saw al-Razi said after mentioning the author's definition: As for the fornicator being legally responsible, willing, and the woman being desired, these are conditions for applying the ruling to them. And the commentator's statement 'if he had defined it as he said, it would be more complete' means more fulfilling of the conditions. Yes, it remains that it must be in dar al-Islam, so that if he committed zina in dar al-harb, there is no hadd, as will come. And this condition the author hinted at with his saying 'and its place' - his saying: (And he responded to it in Fath al-Qadir, etc.) He mentioned it in al-Fath in the coming chapter at his saying: 'And if he had intercourse with his brother's or uncle's slave girl and said 'I thought she was permissible for me,' he is given the hadd.' He said: That is, if he knows that zina is forbidden but thought that his intercourse with this one is not forbidden zina, it does not contradict what is in al-Muhit that says the hadd requires knowledge that zina is forbidden. Rather, it negates the case of the harbi when he enters dar al-Islam, up to the end of 'condition for obligation.'
Source pages p. 5

Commentary
This long passage is a detailed commentary on the definition of zina (fornication/adultery) as stated in the matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq: 'Zina is intercourse in the front while free from ownership and its semblance.' The author explores both the linguistic and legal definitions, noting that they coincide. Excluded are anal intercourse, intercourse with one's wife or slave woman, and cases of shubha (semblance of ownership). Interestingly, a father's intercourse with his son's slave girl is considered zina in the shar'i sense (though no hadd applies) because the accuser of such an act is not liable for false accusation (qadhf). The commentary then addresses a potential objection: since women are called fornicatresses (zaniyah) in the Quran, their act must be considered zina in reality, contrary to the earlier claim that it is metaphorical. The author argues that the woman's 'enabling' (tamkin) is included in the definition, making her participation actual zina. The passage then criticizes al-Zayla'i's definition for being incomplete both in inclusion (it excludes cases like madness) and exclusion (it does not cover the woman's zina). A more precise definition is given: the zina that obligates hadd is the vaginal intercourse of a legally responsible (mukallaf), willing person with a desired female, without any property right or shubha, in dar al-Islam, or the enabling of that. Additionally, knowledge of the prohibition is a condition for the hadd, as per the narration of 'Umar. The text also discusses whether being in dar al-Islam substitutes for actual knowledge, concluding it does not for the hadd, but may create a shubha. The passage ends with a reference to Fath al-Qadir that a harbi (non-Muslim from dar al-harb) who enters dar al-Islam and commits zina may still be subject to hadd if he knows the prohibition, as zina is forbidden in all religions.

Key Terms
زنا (zina): Legal term for illicit sexual intercourse; defined in the shar'i sense as any prohibited intercourse, but for hadd it requires specific conditions.
تمكين (tamkin): The act of enabling intercourse; for a woman, this is considered her act of zina even without actual penetration.
شبهة (shubha): A semblance of right or doubt that negates the hadd, such as ignorance of prohibition or a mistaken belief of ownership.
مكلف (mukallaf): A legally responsible person, i.e., an adult of sound mind who has reached puberty and is sane.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT a condition for the hadd of zina in the Hanafi school?
A) The act must occur in dar al-Islam.
B) The woman must be a Muslim.
C) The person must have knowledge of the prohibition.
D) The person must be legally responsible (mukallaf).

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school defines the zina that obligates hadd narrowly with specific conditions, emphasizing that ignorance of the prohibition prevents punishment.

Definition of Zina Necessitating Hadd and Condition of Knowledge
Critique of al-Zayla'i's definition and elaboration of conditions for hadd

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of zina that necessitates hadd and explain the condition of knowledge of prohibition, citing the report of Umar.

Core Idea
Knowledge of prohibition is a condition for hadd — ignorance averts the fixed punishment.

In this passage, the author of al-Bahr al-Raiq refines the definition of zina that incurs the hadd penalty. He critiques al-Zayla'i's earlier definition for omitting crucial conditions, particularly the requirement that the offender knows the act is forbidden. Understanding this condition is essential for correctly applying the hadd punishment in Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested definition
Read al-Zayla'i's definition of 'zina that necessitates hadd' and note the missing conditions the author points out.
Al-Zayla'i defined it simply as intercourse of a mukallaf in the front of a desired female without ownership or its semblance willingly.
2
Recognize the conditions added by the author
List the conditions: mukallaf, willing, desired female, in Dar al-Islam, no shubhat milk, and knowledge of prohibition.
The author also includes tamkin (enabling) by either party.
3
Understand the condition of knowledge
Read the report of Umar: differentiate between one who knows and one who does not. Identify how this serves as evidence.
The hadd is waived if the person did not know zina is forbidden.
4
Apply the condition to edge cases
Consider the case of a harbi convert who claims ignorance. The author notes that such a claim is not accepted because zina is universally prohibited.
This shows that knowledge may be imputed when the prohibition is universally known.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Synthesize the author's definition and conditions as the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school.
The definition includes tamkin and the condition of knowledge.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling_with_proof that clarifies the definition of zina that necessitates the hadd punishment and the conditions required for its application. The author critiques al-Zayla'i's definition, arguing that it is incomplete because it fails to account for cases like enabling (tamkin) and the condition of knowledge of prohibition. He provides a more comprehensive definition: zina that incurs hadd is the act of a legally responsible (mukallaf) person, willingly, inserting the glans into the front or anus of a desired female, without any semblance of ownership, occurring in Dar al-Islam, or the enabling of such an act. Additionally, a condition is that the person must know that zina is forbidden, based on the report from Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) that differentiates between one who knows and one who does not. The author also discusses the role of prevalence and propagation in establishing knowledge, noting that mere residence in Dar al-Islam does not automatically fulfill this condition. The commentary on Fath al-Qadeer and al-Nahr further refines the definition and addresses apparent conflicts, particularly regarding a harbi who converts and claims ignorance. The final ruling is that hadd is only obligatory when all conditions, including knowledge of prohibition, are present.

Key Terms
الزنا الموجب للحد (al-zina al-mujib li-l-hadd): The specific act of unlawful sexual intercourse that incurs the fixed punishment (hadd), distinguished from zina in a general sense.
تمكين (tamkin): Enabling the act of zina by allowing oneself to be used for intercourse, which incurs hadd even if one is passive.
شبهة الملك (shubhat al-milk): A semblance or doubt of ownership that prevents the hadd from being applied, such as intercourse with a slave one mistakenly believes is owned.

The Hanafi school requires both the act of zina in Dar al-Islam and the offender's knowledge of its prohibition for the hadd to be applied.

Proof of Zina: Four Witnesses Must Say 'Zina'
Ruling that zina is proven by four witnesses using the word 'zina'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the exact wording required from witnesses to prove zina in a Hanafi court and apply this rule to distinguish valid from invalid testimony.

Core Idea
Zina is proven only by four male witnesses explicitly saying 'zina' — no other word suffices.

This rule may seem strict, but it reflects the Quranic emphasis on certainty in hudud cases. The act of zina must be named directly, not hinted at. This protects both the accused from vague accusations and the legal process from ambiguity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being proven? Zina. How is it proven? By testimony. What is the ruling? The hadd becomes wajib upon valid proof.
2
Identify the conditions of valid testimony
The testimony must come from four male witnesses. They must use the specific Arabic word 'zina'. Any other word like 'wat' (intercourse) or 'jima'' (copulation) is not acceptable.
This condition is unique to zina; other crimes may accept descriptive testimony.
3
Locate the dalil (Quranic evidence)
The author cites two verses: Surah al-Nisa (4:15) and Surah al-Nur (24:4). Both require four witnesses for zina.
The verses are from the Quran, so thubut is qati'.
4
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah (how the dalil supports the ruling)
The verses command four witnesses. The Hanafi school infers that the testimony must be about zina itself, not about a general act of intercourse. This inference is based on the context and the severity of the punishment.
The wording of the testimony is part of the requirement derived from the Quran.
5
Apply the rule to a sample case
If four men testify: 'We saw him commit zina', it is accepted. If they say: 'We saw them having intercourse', it is rejected.
This is the practical consequence of the rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بشهادة أربعة بالزنا لا بالوطء والجماع أي يثبت الزنا عند الحاكم ظاهراً بشهادة أربعة من الرجال يشهدون بلفظ الزنا لا بلفظ الوطء والجماع لقوله تعالى: ﴿فَاسْتَشْهِدُوا عَلَيْهِنَّ أَرْبَعَةً مِنْكُمْ﴾ [النساء: 15] وقال تعالى: ﴿ثُمَّ لَمْ يَأْتُوا بِأَرْبَعَةِ شُهَدَاءَ﴾ [النور: 4]
By testimony of four [witnesses] using [the word] 'zina', not by 'intercourse' or 'copulation'. That is, zina is proven before the judge apparently by the testimony of four men who testify using the word 'zina', not using the word 'intercourse' or 'copulation', due to His saying: 'Then call to witness four witnesses from among you' [al-Nisa: 15] and His saying: 'Then if they do not bring four witnesses' [al-Nur: 4].
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage is a ruling_with_proof stating that zina is proven in court specifically by the testimony of four male witnesses who must explicitly use the word 'zina' (al-zina). The Quranic verses cited require four witnesses for proving zina, and the Hanafi understanding is that the witnesses must directly refer to the act of zina itself, not merely describe intercourse or copulation. The wording is critical because the hadd punishment is severe and must be established with clarity. The author does not accept testimony using vague terms like 'wat' (intercourse) or 'jima'' (copulation). This is to ensure the highest level of certainty and to protect against false accusations.

Key Terms
شهادة (shahada): Testimony in court; specifically for zina, it requires four male witnesses using the word 'zina'.
زنا (zina): The act of unlawful sexual intercourse; in legal context, it must be explicitly stated by witnesses.

Ignorance of Prohibition as a Defense Against Hadd
Ruling on ignorance of zina prohibition for new converts vs lifelong Muslims

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between cases where ignorance of the prohibition of zina is accepted as a defense against hadd and where it is not, according to Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
Credible ignorance of prohibition exempts from hadd for new Muslims but not lifelong Muslims.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, Ibn Nujaym addresses a critical question: can a person who commits zina claim they did not know it was haram to avoid punishment? The answer is nuanced and hinges on whether the person is a lifelong Muslim or a recent convert. The passage also clarifies the legal meaning of 'obligation' in hadd punishments, distinguishing between the imam's duty and the sinner's obligation before Allah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage discusses two cases: a non-Muslim who converts and commits zina claiming ignorance, and a lifelong Muslim who claims ignorance. Note the different rulings for each.
The text initially states the convert is flogged without accepting ignorance, then later discusses the lifelong Muslim scenario.
2
Understand the condition of knowledge
The passage explains that the condition for hadd is knowledge of prohibition at the time of the act. For a lifelong Muslim, this knowledge is assumed; for a new convert, it may be absent.
Knowledge is a condition for taklif (legal responsibility).
3
Analyze the author's objection and response
The author raises an objection: if knowledge is a condition for actual obligation, then a lifelong Muslim who claims ignorance should not be flogged. The response clarifies that 'obligation in actual fact' refers to the imam's duty once proof is established, not the sinner's self-punishment.
The imam's duty is wajib; the sinner's ignorance does not negate the proof's existence.
4
Apply the ruling from al-Zahiriyyah
The passage cites a subsidiary ruling: if an insane person commits zina during a lucid interval, he is flogged; but if he claims it was during insanity, he is not. Similarly, a minor's claim of childhood is accepted.
This reinforces that legal capacity (taklif) is a condition for hadd.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دخل دار الإسلام فأسلم فزنى وقال ظننت أنه حلال يحد ولا يلتفت إليه وإن كان فعله أول دخوله فكيف يقال إذا ادعى مسلم أصلي أنه لا يعلم حرمة الزنا أنه لا يحد لانتفاء شرط يوم الحد، ولو أنه أراد أن المعنى أن شرط الحد في نفس الأمر علمه بالحرمة في نفس الأمر فإذا لم يكن عالماً لا حد عليه كان قليل الجدوى أو غير صحيح لأن الشرع لما أوجب على الإمام أن يحد هذا الرجل الذي ثبت زناه عنده عرف ثبوت الوجوب في نفس الأمر لأنه لا معنى لكونه واجباً في نفس الأمر لأنه يكفيه فيما بينه وبين الله تعالى التوبة والإنابة، ثم إذا اتصل بالإمام ثبوته وجب على الإمام إقامة الحد ا.هـ. وفي الظهيرية : والذي يجن ويفيق إذا زنا في حال إفاقته أخذ بالحد ، وإن قال زنيت في حال جنوني لا يحد كالبالغ إذا قال زنيت في حال الصبا.
He entered Dar al-Islam and became Muslim, then committed zina and said 'I thought it was halal.' He is to be flogged and no attention is paid to his claim. But if his action was at the beginning of his entry, how can it be said that if an original Muslim claims he does not know the prohibition of zina, he is not flogged due to the absence of the condition on the day of flogging? If he meant that the condition of flogging in actual fact is his knowledge of the prohibition in actual fact, then if he was not knowledgeable, there would be no flogging; it is of little benefit or incorrect because the Shari'ah, when it obligated the imam to flog this man whose zina has been established before him, it is known that the obligation is established in actual fact, because there is no meaning to its being obligatory in actual fact since it suffices him between him and Allah Almighty to repent and return. Then when its establishment reaches the imam, it becomes obligatory on the imam to execute the flogging. End. And in al-Zahiriyyah: One who alternately becomes insane and sane, if he commits zina during his sanity, he is subject to flogging; if he says 'I committed zina during my insanity', he is not flogged, like an adult if he says 'I committed zina during childhood'.
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage, from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, addresses the condition of knowledge of prohibition as a prerequisite for the hadd punishment for zina. The author discusses a scenario where a non-Muslim enters Dar al-Islam, converts, and then commits zina claiming he thought it was halal. The initial ruling states that he is flogged and his ignorance is not accepted. However, a distinction is made if the act occurred immediately upon entry. The text then raises an objection: if a native-born Muslim claims ignorance of the prohibition of zina, he is not flogged because the condition of knowledge is absent on the day of punishment. The author analyzes the meaning of 'obligatory in actual fact'—it is not that the sinner must punish himself, but rather that the obligation to punish exists in the sight of Allah, and the imam's duty to execute is triggered by established proof. The passage concludes with a ruling from al-Zahiriyyah: an insane person who commits zina during a lucid interval is flogged, but if he claims it was during insanity, he is not flogged, similar to a minor.

Key Terms
الحد (al-hadd): The fixed corporal punishment prescribed by Shari'ah for certain crimes, here referring to flogging for zina.
دار الإسلام (Dar al-Islam): Territory where Islamic law is predominant and Muslims are safe to practice their religion.
مسلم أصلي (muslim asli): A lifelong Muslim who was born into Islam and raised in an Islamic environment, as opposed to a new convert.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling for a lifelong Muslim who claims he did not know zina was haram?
A) He is not flogged because ignorance is always an excuse.
B) He is flogged because his ignorance is not credible.
C) He is given a discretionary punishment (ta'zir).
D) He is required to repent only.

Key Takeaway
Credible ignorance of the prohibition of zina is a valid defense against hadd for new Muslims but not for lifelong Muslims, based on the condition of knowledge at the time of the act.
